Source: BPF Internals – II | Tuxology - WordPress.com
URL: https://suchakra.wordpress.com/2015/08/12/bpf-internals-ii/
BPF Internals – II | Tuxology Tuxology Tweets Tweets by tuxology Android Embedded GDB India Java Kernel Linux Perf Philosophy Poems Python Qt Raspberry Pi Synths UX/UI Twitter LinkedIn GitHub Search Search Widgets Connect Search Tuxology Menu Skip to content Home About Linux BPF Internals – II August 12, 2015 suchakra BPF, eBPF, linux Leave a comment Continuing from where I left before, in this post we would see some of the major changes in BPF that have happened recently – how it is evolving to be a very stable and accepted in-kernel VM and can probably be the next big thing – in not just filtering but going beyond. From what I observe, the most attractive feature of BPF is its ability to give access to the developers so that they can execute dynamically compiled code within the kernel – in a limited context, but still securely. This itself is a valuable asset. As we have seen already, the use of BPF is not just limited to filtering out network packets but for seccomp, tracing etc. The eventual step for BPF in such a scenario was to evolve and come out of it's use in the network filtering world. To improve the architecture and bytecode, lots of additions have been proposed. I started a bit late when I saw Alexei's patches for kernel version 3.17-rcX. Perhaps, this was the relevant mail by Alexei that got me interested in the upcoming changes. So, here is a summary of what all major changes have occured. We will be seeing each of them in sufficient detail. Advertisement Architecture The classic BPF we discussed in the last post had two 32 bit registers – A and X. All arithmetic operations were supported and performed using these two registers. The newer BPF called extended-BPF or eBPF has ten 64 bit registers and supports arbitary load/stores. It also contains new instructions like BPF_CALL which can be used to call some new kernel-side helper functions. We will look into this in detail a bit later as well. The new eBPF follows calling conventions which are more like modern machines (x86_64). Here is the mapping of the new eBPF registers to x86 registers : Advertisement The closeness to the machine ABI also ensures that unnecessary register spilling/copying can be avoided. The R0 register stores the return from the eBPF program and the eBPF program contexts can be loaded through register R1. Earlier, there used to be just two jump targets i.e. either jump to TRUE or FALSE targets. Now, there can be arbitary jump targets – true or fall through. Another aspect of the eBPF instruction set is the ease of use with the in-kernel JIT compiler. eBPF Registers and most instructions are now mapped one-to-one. This makes emitting these eBPF instructions from any external compiler (in userspace) not such a daunting task. Of course, prior to any execution, the generated bytecode is passed through a verifier in the kernel to check its sanity. The verifier in itself is a very interesting and important piece of code and probably story for another day. Building BPF Programs Advertisement From a users perspective, the new eBPF bytecode can now be another headache to generate. But fear not, an LLVM based backend now supports instructions being generated for BPF pseudo-machine type directly. It is being 'graduated' from just being an experimental backend and can hit the shelf anytime soon. In the meantime, you can always use this script to setup the BPF supported LLVM yourslef. But, then what next? So, a BPF program (not necessarily just a filter anymore) can be done in two parts – A kernel part (the BPF bytecode which will get loaded in the kernel) and the userspace part (which may, if needed gather data from the kernel part) Currently you can specify a eBPF program in a restricted C like language. For example, here is a program in the restricted C which returns true if the first argument of the input program context is 42. Nothing fancy : Advertisement This C like syntax generates a BPF binary which can then be loaded in the kernel. Here is what it looks like in BPF 'assembly' representation as generated by the LLVM backed (supplied with 3.4) : If you are adventerous enough, you can also probably write complete and valid BPF programs in assembly in a single go – right from your userspace program. I do not know if this is of any use these days. I have done this sometime back for a moderately elaborate trace filtering program though. It is also not effective as well, becasue I think at this point in human history, LLVM can generate assembly better and more efficiently than a human. Advertisement What we discussed just now is probably not a relevant program anymore. An example by Alexei here is what is more relevant these days. With the integration of Kprobe with BPF, a BPF program can be run at any valid dynamically instrumentable function in the kernel. So now, we can probably just use pt_regs as the context and get individual register values at each time the probe is hit. As of now, some helper functions are available in BPF as well, which can get the current timestamp. You can have a very cheap tracing tool right there 🙂 BPF Maps I think one of the most interesting features in this new eBPF is the BPF maps. It looks like an abstract data type – initially a hash-table, but from kernel 3.19 onwards, support for array-maps seems to have been added as well. These bpf_maps can be used to store data generated from a eBPF program being executed. You can see the implementation details in arraymap.c or hashtab.c Lets pause for a while and see some more magic added in eBPF – esp. the BPF syscall which forms the primary interface for the user to interact and use eBPF. The reason we want to know more about this syscall is to know how to work with these cool BPF maps. Advertisement BPF Syscall Another nice thing about eBPF is a new syscall being added to make life easier while dealing with BPF programs. In an article last year on LWN Jonathan Corbet discussed the use of BPF syscall. For example, to load a BPF program you could call with of course, the corresponding bpf_attr structure being filled before : Advertisement Yes, this may seem cumbersome to some, so for now, there are some wrapper functions in bpf_load.c and libbpf.c released to help folks out where you may need not give too many details about your compiled bpf program. Much of what happens in the BPF syscall is determined by the arguments supported here. To elaborate more, let's see how to load the BPF program we did before. Assuming that we have the sample program in its BPF bytecode form generated and now we want to load it up, we take the help of the wrapper function load_bpf_file() which parses the BPF ELF file and extracts the BPF bytecode from the relevant section. It also iterates over all ELF sections to get licence info, map info etc. Eventually, as per the type of BPF program – Kprobre/kretprobe or socket program, and the info and bytecode just gathered from the ELF parsing, the bpf_attr attribute structure is filled and actual syscall is made. Creating and accessing BPF maps Advertisement Coming back to the maps, apart from this simple syscall to load the BPF program, there are many more actions that can be taken based on just the arguments. Have a look at bpf/syscall.c From the userspace side the new BPF syscall comes to the rescue and allows most of these operations on bpf_maps to be performed! From the kernel side however, with some special helper function and the use of BPF_CALL instruction, the values in these maps can be updated/deleted/accessed etc. These helpers inturn call the actual function according to the type of map – hash-map or an array. For example, here is a BPF program that just creates an array-map and does nothing else, Advertisement When loaded in the kernel, the array-map is created. Form the userspace we can then probably initialize the map with some values with a function that look likes this, where bpf_update_elem() wrapper is in-turn calling the BPF syscall with proper arguments and attributes as, This inturn calls map_update_elem() which securely copies the key and value using copy_from_user() and then calls the specialized function for updating the value for array-map at the specified index. Similar things happen for reading/deleting/creating has or array maps from userspace. Advertisement So probably, things will start falling into pieces now from the earlier post by Brendan Gregg where he was updating a map from the BPF program (using the BPF CALL instruction which calls the internal kernel helpers) and then concurrently accessing it from userspace to generate a beautiful histogram (through the syscall I just mentioned above). BPF Maps are indeed a very powerful addition to the system. You can also checkout more detailed and complete examples now that you know what is going on. To summarize, this is how an example BPF program written in restricted C for kernel part and normal C for userspace part would run these days: In the next BPF post, I will discuss the eBPF verifier in detail. This is the most crucial part of BPF and deserves detailed attention I think. There is also something cool happening these days on the Plumgrid side I think – the BPF Compiler Collection. There was a very interesting demo using such tools and the power of eBPF at the recent Red Hat Summit. I got BCC working and tried out some examples with probes – where I could easily compile and load BPF programs from my Python scripts! How cool is that 🙂 Also, I have been digging through the LTTng's interpreter lately so probably another post detailing how the BPF and LTTng's interpreters work would be nice to know. That's all for now. Run BPF. Share this: Share on Facebook (Opens in new window) Facebook Share on X (Opens in new window) X Email a link to a friend (Opens in new window) Email Like Loading... Source: BPF Verifier State Pruning: Timeline | pchaigno
URL: https://pchaigno.github.io/ebpf/2026/01/20/state-pruning-timeline.html
BPF Verifier State Pruning: Timeline | pchaigno pchaigno [-] About Talks BPF Verifier State Pruning: Timeline Published on Jan 20, 2026 This article is part of a series of notes that Mahé Tardy and I wrote to prepare a presentation introducing the BPF verifier state pruning for Linux Plumbers 2025 in Tokyo. You can also find the slides and the video recording of the presentation. State pruning is what helps the BPF verifier in Linux scale to larger programs. It mitigates the path explosion problem by pruning paths that are equivalent to already-verified paths. State pruning evolved alongside the verifier for the past decade. As we illustrated in our talk, it started as a simple optimization and grew into a more complex and efficient component of the verifier. This timeline tracks the main changes state pruning went through. Each commit is prefixed with a symbol to indicate the overall impact the change had on the complexity (the “cost” of verification). For example, ↓ means the commit decreased complexity and therefore helped the verifier scale. Commits suffixed with a * have been backported to at least one LTS kernel. As we publish more articles with Mahé, I'll include more link to Read more on important changes. v3.18, 2014 17a526 Initial verifier logic. f1bca8 Initial state pruning logic. v4.7, 2016 070161 Add a pruning point after call instructions, in addition to existing pruning points on conditional jump target and after BPF_JA . Read more v4.11, 2017 f0318d Track offseted map value pointers while on the stack. v4.12, 2017 3c2ce6* Add a pruning point on conditional jump instructions. Read more v4.14, 2017 dc503a Track register liveness. v4.16, 2017 2f18f6 Fix liveness propagation in case of register with a pointer filled from the stack. 3bf159 Detect dead BPF_JNE branches. f4d7e4 Verification of subprogs (BPF-to-BPF function calls). cc2b14 Recognize zero-initialized stack slots. v4.20, 2018 4f7b3e* Extend dead-branch detection to all types of numeric conditions. v5.0, 2018 e434b8* Track scalars through 32-bit assignments. 19e2db Don't assume a larger stack means verifier states aren't equivalent. v5.2, 2019 9f4686 Least-recently used mechanism to limit number of saved states at pruning points. v5.3, 2019 f7cf25* Track constant scalar registers as they are spilled to the stack. 258972 Support bounded loops, introduce heuristic to decide when to save the verifier state on a pruning point. b5dc01 Introduce precise tracking of scalar values. a3ce68 Fix precision propagation in case of pruned paths. 675417 Fix precise tracking in case of subprogs. v5.5, 2019 cc52d9 Require precise tracking of map lookup key for tail calls. v5.6, 2020 51c39b Support function-by-function verification. v5.10, 2020 757488 Allocate an ID for scalars to propagate infered ranges to identical scalars. 5689d4 Track 64-bit bounded scalar registers as they are spilled to the stack. v5.15, 2021 bfc6bb Add a pruning point on calls to asynchronous callback functions. Read more v5.16, 2021 354e8f* Track all bounded scalar registers as they are spilled to the stack. v6.2, 2022 a3b666* Fix precision propagation in case of ALU operations. be2ef8* Do not completely disable precise tracking whenever subprogs are used. f63181* Improve accuracy of precision propagation. bffdea* Decouple jump history from pruning points. 7a830b* Improve accuracy of precision propagation by actively forgetting precise marks. v6.3, 2022–2023 4633a0 Fix register comparison in state pruning to take into account ID remapping between paths. 6715df* Relax slack slot equivalence when running with CAP_PERFMON . v6.4, 2023 4b5ce5 Introduce force pruning points for iter_next kfuncs. 13fbce Improve BPF_JEQ and BPF_JNE dead-branch detection. v6.5, 2023 407958 Introduce struct backtrack_state to track the precise marking through backtracking. fde2a3 Support precise tracking for subprogs, including callback functions. 904e6d Share precise mark between all scalars with the same ID. v6.7, 2023 2793a8* For iter_next loops, require exact state match in state pruning and introduce widening of registers. 42d31d Improve BPF_JEQ and BPF_JNE dead-branch detection by using signed ranges. ab5cfa* Add a pruning point on calls to synchronous callback functions, fix callback function verification to verify all iterations. Read more cafe2c* Extend use of register widening to synchronous callback functions. v6.8, 2023 0acd03* Require precise tracking of R0 on callback function return. eabe51 Require precise tracking of R0 when checking return code is within expected range. 41f6f6 Precise tracking on spill to the stack even if using non-R10 register. 18a433 Don't trigger precise tracking whenever writing zero register to aligned stack slots. v6.9, 2024 9a4c57 Don't trigger precise tracking whenever writing zero immediate to aligned stack slots, a pattern common for mcpu=v4 . 6efbde Improve state pruning when comparing unbounded spilled register to misc. stack slots. v6.12, 2024 4bf79f Improving precise tracking at conditional jumps in case of linked registers. v6.15, 2025 14c855 Data-flow analysis for register liveness, before the actual program analysis. v6.17, 2025 96c6aa Compute Strongly Connected Components (SCCs) of control-flow graph. c9e319 Use SCC to improve read and precise marks propagation in case of loops. v6.18, 2025 b3698c Introduce path-insensitive data flow analysis for liveness tracking. f41345* Use tnum information to improve BPF_JEQ and BPF_JNE dead-branch detection. pchaigno pchaigno paul.chaignon@gmail.com pchaigno pchaigno pchaigno Paul Chaignon's blog

Up Arrow Back to Blog Product By Leonid Frenkel Debugging eBPF verifier: Best Practices for High-Performance Kernel Programming Introduction eBPF (Extended Berkeley Packet Filter) is a powerful technology that enables safe and efficient execution of custom programs within the Linux kernel. It has been widely adopted for networking, observability, and security use cases. However, eBPF programs are subject to various constraints to ensure performance and security. One of the key limitations is the maximum instruction count, which can pose challenges when developing complex eBPF applications. The eBPF Instruction Limit As of recent Linux kernel versions, eBPF programs are limited to 1,000,000 instructions per execution path. This limit is enforced by the eBPF verifier, which ensures that programs are safe and do not lead to unbounded execution, infinite loops, or excessive CPU consumption. Historically, earlier kernel versions imposed a stricter limit of 4,096 instructions per program. This was later increased to 1,000,000 to accommodate more complex use cases such as advanced tracing, security monitoring, and in-kernel processing. Why Does This Limitation Exist? The instruction count limitation exists primarily for the following reasons: Performance and Latency: Since eBPF programs execute within the kernel, excessive computation could lead to increased latency or resource exhaustion. Safety and Termination Guarantee: The kernel must ensure that eBPF programs will always terminate and not enter infinite loops, which could crash or hang the system. Verifier Complexity: The eBPF verifier performs static analysis of all possible execution paths to determine whether the program is safe. The instruction limit helps keep this analysis computationally feasible. Implications for Developers For developers working with eBPF, hitting the instruction limit can be a significant challenge, especially when implementing complex logic. Here are some common approaches to mitigate this limitation:
Optimize the eBPF Program Reduce unnecessary instructions and redundant computations. Use compiler optimizations (-O2 or -O3 in Clang) to generate efficient bytecode. Avoid deep function call chains that expand into excessive instructions.
Use Tail Calls eBPF supports tail calls, which allow one program to jump into another without returning. This helps break large programs into multiple smaller ones, avoiding the instruction limit per execution path. However, tail calls are limited to 33 chained calls per execution to prevent infinite recursion.
Leverage Maps for Complex Logic Instead of performing expensive computations in the eBPF program, store data in BPF maps and perform processing in user space. Hash maps, array maps, and ring buffers can help offload heavy operations.
Minimize Loop Iterations eBPF supports bounded loops, but their iteration count is analyzed by the verifier to prevent unbounded execution. Reducing loop complexity and iterations helps stay within the instruction limit.
Use Helper Functions eBPF provides a set of helper functions that execute optimized kernel operations (e.g., accessing packet data, retrieving timestamps, etc.). Using these helpers instead of custom logic can save valuable instructions.
Measuring eBPF Verifier Instruction Count Using eBPF The eBPF verifier only provides feedback when a program reaches the 1,000,000 instruction limit, preventing successful loading. However, developers may still want to track the instruction count of successfully loaded programs to analyze the impact of code changes. To achieve this, a simple eBPF program can attach kprobe and kretprobe to the do_check function (static int do_check(struct bpf_verifier_env *env)). The kprobe captures the pointer to env, and the kretprobe extracts and prints relevant details, including: Instruction count: env->insn_processed Program name: env->prog->aux->name Program length: env->prog->len With this approach, developers can continuously measure how changes to their eBPF programs impact the verifier's instruction count, enabling more precise optimizations, eliminating instruction limit surprises Conclusion The eBPF instruction count limitation is a fundamental constraint designed to balance performance, security, and feasibility of verification. While the current 1,000,000 instruction limit is sufficient for most use cases, developers must be mindful of it when writing complex eBPF programs. By leveraging optimizations, tail calls, maps, helper functions and measuring instructions count, developers can work within this constraint to build efficient and scalable eBPF-based solutions. Source: Demystifying eBPF: In-Kernel Virtualization, Verification Safety, and Subsystem Hooking Architectures
Type: Pasted Text
Demystifying eBPF: In-Kernel Virtualization, Verification Safety, and Subsystem Hooking ArchitecturesThe historical architecture of the Linux kernel forced developers into a difficult trade-off: write raw kernel modules to achieve high performance while risking system stability, or write user-space applications that guarantee safety but incur high context-switching and data-copying overhead.[1, 2] The introduction of the Extended Berkeley Packet Filter (eBPF) resolved this tension.[1, 3] By implementing a fully programmable, sandboxed execution environment directly within the privileged kernel space, eBPF allows custom logic to run dynamically at runtime.[1, 3] This capability extends operating system behavior safely and efficiently, eliminating the need to modify kernel source code or load untested kernel modules.[1, 4]
The In-Kernel Virtual Machine: Registers, Calling Conventions, and InstructionsThe core of eBPF is a register-based virtual machine running inside the Linux kernel.[5, 6] The architecture of this virtual machine matches modern hardware instruction sets to minimize the instruction translation overhead of the Just-In-Time (JIT) compiler.[7, 8]Register Allocation and MappingThe eBPF execution model defines eleven 64-bit registers, designated as R0 through R10, along with a dedicated program counter.[7] For compatibility with 32-bit CPU architectures and narrow arithmetic operations, the virtual machine exposes 32-bit lower subregisters.[7, 9] Whenever a 32-bit subregister is written to, the higher 32 bits are zero-extended into the corresponding 64-bit register.[7, 9]This calling convention is designed to map directly to the Application Binary Interfaces (ABIs) of modern 64-bit architectures like x86_64, arm64, and mips64.[7, 9] Because the virtual machine register definitions align with physical CPU registers, JIT compilers can translate eBPF instructions directly into native machine code without executing complex register-shuffling routines.[7]Instruction Encoding and Safe Kernel HelpersThe eBPF virtual machine uses a fixed-size 64-bit instruction encoding scheme.[7] Every instruction is formatted as a two-operand operation to simplify translation to native instruction sets.[7] A single 64-bit instruction is structured from most significant bit to least significant bit as:op:8 — The operation code specifying the instruction class, source operand type (register or immediate), and specific operation.[7]dst_reg:4 — The destination register operand (R0 - R10).[7]src_reg:4 — The source register operand (R0 - R10).[7]off:16 — A signed relative offset, used for addressing stack memory, map offsets, or jump targets.[7]imm:32 — A signed immediate constant value.[7]To prevent arbitrary execution and ensure compatibility across kernel versions, eBPF programs cannot call arbitrary kernel functions.[3] Instead, they invoke helper functions through a stable API provided by the kernel.[3] These helpers allow programs to safely perform operations such as looking up system clock times, generating pseudo-random numbers, modifying packet data, and interacting with shared storage maps.[3, 12]
The Static Bytecode Verifier: Mechanisms of Safety and State PruningThe primary defense mechanism for kernel safety is the eBPF static verifier.[4, 13] Before any bytecode program can be JIT-compiled and attached to a kernel hook, the verifier performs a rigorous safety analysis to ensure the code cannot destabilize or crash the operating system.[3, 4]The Dual-Pass Verification ModelThe verifier runs two distinct validation passes over the submitted eBPF program:Control Flow Graph Validation (check_cfg()): The first pass parses the program's control flow graph using a depth-first search (DFS).[14, 15] This pass guarantees program termination by verifying that all loops are strictly bounded with known exit conditions and ensures that there are no unreachable instructions (unreachable insn errors).[3, 13]State Transition Simulation (do_check()): The second pass simulates the execution of every instruction, tracking the state transitions of all registers and stack slots.[16, 17] This pass ensures that no uninitialized variables are read (! read_ok errors), memory accesses do not exceed strict boundaries, and pointers are not treated as scalar values.[4, 13, 16]State Representation and Boundary TrackingThe verifier tracks state using struct bpf_reg_state, which unifies scalar and pointer representation.[16, 17] Registers are assigned specific types (such as PTR_TO_CTX for the program context, PTR_TO_MAP_VALUE for map values, or PTR_TO_SOCKET for socket lookups).[16, 17] Pointers returned from map or socket lookups are initially tracked as nullable (PTR_TO_MAP_VALUE_OR_NULL), forcing the programmer to implement explicit null checks.[16, 17] These pointers share a unique ID; once a null check is executed in one branch, the verifier promotes all registers sharing that ID to non-nullable types.[16, 17]For scalar tracking, the verifier monitors the range of possible values within each register using signed and unsigned minimum and maximum bounds (umin_value, umax_value, smin_value, smax_value).[10, 16] When conditional branches are evaluated, the boundaries of these values are updated.[16] For example, if a scalar register is checked via an instruction like if R2 > 8 goto..., the "true" path updates umin_value to 9, while the "false" path restricts umax_value to 8.[16]State Pruning, Liveness, and BacktrackingTo prevent path explosion during simulation of complex programs, the verifier relies on state pruning.[15, 18]Pruning Points: Pruning points are established at unconditional jump targets (BPF_JA), conditional jumps, function calls, and helper/callback boundaries.[15, 18] When the verifier hits a pruning point, it compares its current state (registers and stack) with a list of previously verified states.[18] If the current state is mathematically "included" (\subseteq) within a saved state—meaning its ranges are subset to the verified ranges—the path is pruned, halting redundant analysis.[15, 18]Liveness Analysis: To optimize state comparisons, a data-flow pre-pass calculates register and stack "liveness".[18] A register is "dead" if its current value is overwritten before being read.[18] The verifier ignores the values of dead registers and unread stack slots during equivalence checks, accelerating verification.[18]Precise Tracking: The verifier avoids tracking the precise values of scalar registers unless they are used to make safety-critical decisions (such as serving as a map lookup key, an array index, or a helper buffer size argument).[18] When such an event occurs, the register is marked as "precise".[18] This precise flag is then backtracked through the program's jump history to previous states, ensuring that only necessary scalar variables are compared during state pruning.[18]This verification engine has undergone significant iterations to expand complexity limits and scale execution safety:Cooperative Runtime EnforcementDespite the verifier's evolution, strict static verification often rejects valid programs due to the halting problem or limited reasoning about complex memory structures.[19] To resolve these limitations, frameworks such as ePass introduce cooperative runtime enforcement.[19] By utilizing a Verifier Information (VI) map, the in-kernel verifier exports its analysis results (including register types and range boundaries) to a user-space engine.[19] The user-space compiler can analyze why a program was rejected (e.g., due to unprovable loop bounds or complex pointer arithmetic) and insert small runtime checks or transformation bytecode (such as loop counters or memory boundary masks) to make the program verifiable.[19] The transformed bytecode is then resubmitted for verification, maintaining the kernel's security model.[19]
JIT Compilation, Hardening, and Read-Only ProtectionOnce a program successfully passes verification, the generic eBPF bytecode is processed by the host architecture's in-kernel JIT compiler to translate the virtual instructions into native machine code.[1, 4]Machine-Level Translation and SpeedThe JIT compiler maps the virtual register states R0 - R10 directly onto the physical registers of the host CPU.[7, 8] Because eBPF was designed with modern instruction ABIs in mind, this translation requires minimal instruction mapping overhead.[7] This achieves near-native performance, which is essential for processing high-frequency system events and packet-filtering operations at line-rate.[1, 4]Kernel Hardening and Mitigation StrategiesExecuting dynamically loaded machine code inside the kernel's address space introduces critical security surfaces that require runtime hardening [3]:Read-Only Page Protection: Immediately after compilation, the physical kernel memory pages holding the native instructions are marked as read-only and executable (RX).[3] Any attempts to modify these pages (via kernel bugs or malicious writes) trigger an immediate kernel crash, preventing runtime code manipulation.[3]Spectre Mitigations: To prevent speculative execution side-channel exploits, the verifier and JIT engine work together to analyze transient execution paths.[3] Memory access boundaries are masked under speculative states, and the JIT compiler implements Retpolines to secure indirect jumps and tail call tables.[3]Constant Blinding: To prevent JIT spraying attacks—where an attacker loads constants that contain hidden shellcode instructions—all immediate constants inside the eBPF bytecode are blinded (e.g., XORed with a random key) during JIT compilation.[3] The JIT compiler emits code that decodes these constants at runtime, ensuring that raw shellcode bytes do not appear sequentially in executable memory.[3]
eBPF Maps: Shared State Storage and SynchronizationBecause eBPF programs run within isolated, sandboxed environments, they are barred from performing standard inter-process communication (IPC) or accessing arbitrary kernel memory.[1, 3] Instead, they coordinate state and share data with user-space applications and other eBPF programs via optimized key-value data structures known as eBPF Maps.[1, 3]Map Architecture and TypeseBPF maps are defined as native kernel objects.[6] The verifier knows the properties of each map (such as key size, value size, and max entries) at load time, ensuring that all runtime lookup and write operations are boundary-checked and memory-safe.[4] Maps support diverse structures to match different workloads [3]:Hash and Array Maps: General-purpose lookups.[3]Least Recently Used (LRU) Maps: Cache structures that evict old elements when maximum capacity is reached.[3]Ring Buffers: High-throughput queues that support lockless, zero-copy data streaming from the kernel to user space.[3, 20]Longest Prefix Match (LPM) Triemaps: Optimized structures for IP routing lookup tables.[3]Concurrency and Thread SafetyMultiple instances of an eBPF program can execute concurrently across different CPU cores, and user-space processes can read or write to maps simultaneously.[3, 6] To prevent race conditions and locking overhead, map operations are protected using the kernel's Read-Copy Update (RCU) mechanism.[6] For per-CPU maps, independent storage areas are allocated for each core, avoiding cross-CPU cache invalidation and ensuring lock-free performance.[3] User space interacts with these maps using specific bpf() system call subcommands [11]:BPF_MAP_LOOKUP_ELEM — Retrieves a value matching a specified key.[11]BPF_MAP_UPDATE_ELEM — Inserts or updates a key-value pair.[11]BPF_MAP_DELETE_ELEM — Deletes a key-value pair.[11]BPF_MAP_GET_NEXT_KEY — Iterates through map keys.[11]
Dynamic Tracing: Kprobes, Uprobes, and Trapless ArchitecturesDynamic tracing allows developers to attach eBPF handlers to arbitrary functions in a running system without needing recompilation or service restarts.[1, 21]Kernel Probes (Kprobes and Kretprobes)Kprobes enable hooks to be placed at any instruction address inside the kernel.[22, 23]Mechanism: When a kprobe is registered, the kernel copies the original instruction at the target address and overwrites its first byte with a breakpoint trap instruction (such as INT3 on x86_64).[24] When execution reaches this instruction, the CPU triggers a breakpoint exception.[24] The kernel's debug exception handler captures this event, suspends standard execution, and invokes the eBPF handler via the notifier call chain.[24] After the eBPF handler completes, the copied instruction is executed, and execution returns to the original path.[24, 25]Limitations: Kprobes depend on internal kernel symbols found in /proc/kallsyms.[23] Because these symbols can change or be deleted between kernel updates, kprobes have low portability across kernel versions.[22, 23] Additionally, the exception handling workflow introduces significant performance overhead, requiring over 6,000 CPU cycles per probe hit.[25] Kretprobes attach to function exits but lack access to input parameters upon function return.[23]User Probes (Uprobes and Uretprobes)Uprobes apply the breakpoint-injection mechanism to user-space application binaries.[21, 22] The kernel places an INT3 breakpoint at a calculated offset within the virtual address space (Virtual Memory Area/VMA) of the target process.[24] When the application hits the breakpoint, it traps into kernel space to execute the eBPF handler.[2, 21]Because the execution transitions from user-space application context to kernel-space and back, uprobes trigger dual context switches.[2, 26] This introduces substantial latency bottlenecks, making uprobes nearly ten times slower than kprobes.[2]Trapless Probes and User-Space eBPF RuntimesTo bypass the overhead of trap-based dynamic tracing, advanced architectural alternatives are utilized:Trapless Code Rewriting: Compile-time padding can insert 5-byte No-Operation (NOP) instructions before kernel functions.[25] At runtime, these NOPs are overwritten with direct relative jumps to a global trampoline.[25] This trampoline saves CPU registers, executes the handler, and jumps directly back, bypassing exception handling.[25]User-Space runtimes (bpftime): Runtimes like bpftime use user-space binary rewriting to attach uprobes and syscall hooks directly within the application's memory space.[2, 26] By compiling the eBPF program via LLVM JIT/AOT and embedding the virtual machine directly into the function call context of the traced process, bpftime executes the handlers in user space without context switches.[2, 26] This achieves up to a 10x improvement in uprobe execution speed while maintaining compatibility with standard tools like libbpf.[2, 26]
Static Tracing: Tracepoints and FprobesUnlike dynamic probes, static tracing relies on predefined instrumentation points built into the software by developers.[22]Tracepoints (Standard vs. Raw)Tracepoints are static markers compiled into the kernel code using the TRACE_EVENT macro.[23]Standard Tracepoints: When triggered, standard tracepoints pre-process raw function parameters into a structured, typed context struct (such as struct trace_sys_enter_execve).[23] The eBPF program can read these fields directly, ensuring high portability and API stability across different kernel versions.[23, 27] However, parameter formatting introduces processing overhead.[23]Raw Tracepoints: Raw tracepoints bypass parameter pre-processing.[23] Instead of a formatted context, they pass arguments to the eBPF program in their raw register-level format via struct bpf_raw_tracepoint_args.[23] This reduces overhead but requires the developer to manually extract parameters from CPU registers, making raw tracepoint code architecture-specific and less portable.[23, 27] Additionally, raw tracepoints hook into global syscall entry points (sys_enter and sys_exit), requiring manual filtering by syscall ID.[23, 27]Fprobes (Fentry/Fexit)Fprobes overcome the performance limitations of kprobes by leveraging the kernel's compiler-inserted ftrace mechanism.[23]At compile time, a NOP instruction is generated at the entry point of every kernel function.[23]At runtime, the kernel patches this NOP to jump to a specialized eBPF trampoline.[23]This trampoline invokes the eBPF program as if it were a direct function call, bypassing breakpoint exceptions.[23]Fprobes can hook onto function entry (fentry) or exit (fexit) points.[23] Unlike standard kretprobes (which cannot read parameters at function exit), fexit fprobes maintain full access to both the original input arguments and the final return values.[23] Fprobes require a minimum kernel version of 5.5 and are restricted to function boundaries.[23]
eXpress Data Path (XDP): Driver-Level Network OptimizationThe eXpress Data Path (XDP) is a high-performance network processing framework that runs eBPF programs directly within the network driver's receive path.[28, 29]Bypassing the Kernel Network StackStandard Linux network processing requires the driver to allocate a complex socket buffer structure (sk_buff) and run Generic Receive Offload (GRO) before passing packets up the TCP/IP stack.[30, 31] This allocation and parsing process consumes significant CPU cycles.[28, 29, 30]XDP intercepts incoming packets immediately after DMA transfer from the Network Interface Card (NIC), before any sk_buff allocation or kernel protocol processing occurs.[6, 30] It exposes the raw packet bytes to an eBPF program via a lightweight xdp_md context.[30, 32] This early intervention allows XDP to drop or forward packets with minimal CPU usage, making it highly effective for DDoS mitigation, load balancing, and firewalling.[5, 28]XDP Execution ModesXDP operates in three distinct modes depending on hardware and driver support [30, 33]:Offloaded Mode: The eBPF program is compiled into native NIC instruction sets and executed directly on a SmartNIC's ASIC.[30, 33] This achieves wire-speed execution and frees host CPU resources.[5, 33]Native Mode (Driver Level): This is the default mode, where the program executes within the NIC's main device driver thread before entering the kernel networking stack.[30, 33]Generic Mode (SKB Fallback): Used as a fallback when native driver support is unavailable.[30, 34] The program runs after sk_buff has already been allocated.[30, 33] It provides no performance benefits and is primarily used for testing.[30, 33]XDP Return CodesThe eBPF program must return a specific action code to instruct the driver on how to handle the packet [30, 32]:XDP_DROP — Immediately discards the packet, bypassing further processing.[30, 32] Because the packet is dropped before reaching the kernel stack, it is invisible to packet-capturing tools like tcpdump.[32]XDP_PASS — Forwards the packet up to the standard Linux kernel network stack.[30, 32]XDP_TX — Bounces the packet back out the same NIC port it arrived on.[30, 32]XDP_REDIRECT — Forwards the packet to another network interface card, a specific CPU core, or a high-performance AF_XDP user-space socket.[30, 32, 33]XDP_ABORTED — Indicates a program error, triggering a tracepoint exception log for debugging.[30, 32]The performance differences between XDP and traditional Netfilter-based firewalls are significant:
Socket Filtering and Traffic ControlWhen packets proceed past the driver-level XDP hook, they can be monitored or modified at higher layers of the network stack using Socket Filtering or Traffic Control.[31, 35]Socket Filtering (SO_ATTACH_BPF)Socket filters are attached directly to individual sockets using the setsockopt() system call with the SO_ATTACH_BPF option.[11, 36]Execution Context: The eBPF program is called with a pointer to a struct __sk_buff context, which serves as a stable, version-compatible ABI mirror of the kernel's internal sk_buff structure.[11, 37]Copy Semantics: Socket filters run on copies of network packets.[36] If a socket filter returns 0, the packet copy is dropped, but the original packet continues through the kernel's protocol stack unaffected.[36] Returning a non-zero value truncates the copy to that length.[11, 36] Because they operate on copies, socket filters cannot modify or block live transit traffic, making them suitable for application-level observability (such as HTTP header extraction) rather than inline firewalling.[11, 35, 36]Traffic Control (TC BPF)The Linux Traffic Control (TC) subsystem operates at the link layer, running after sk_buff allocation has occurred.[31]Direct Action Mode: Unlike socket filters, TC eBPF programs run on the original packet buffers in transit.[31, 36] They operate in "direct-action" mode, allowing them to return standard TC action codes (such as TC_ACT_OK to pass or TC_ACT_SHOT to drop).[31]Egress Capabilities: While XDP is strictly limited to incoming ingress packets, TC BPF can filter and modify both incoming (ingress) and outgoing (egress) packets, making it highly effective for traffic shaping, packet modification, and container-level network policy enforcement.[28, 31, 36]
Comparative Taxonomy of Hooking and Instrumentation InterfacesTo synthesize these dynamic and static attachment interfaces, the following comparative taxonomy illustrates the trade-offs across execution safety, latency overhead, stability, and typical deployment targets:
Architectural SynthesisThe Extended Berkeley Packet Filter (eBPF) provides a highly performant and secure extension model for the Linux kernel.[1, 3] By using a sandboxed virtual machine, a static verifier, and a JIT compiler, eBPF runs custom code at near-native speeds without compromising system safety.[1, 4]As tracing and networking requirements continue to demand lower latency, the platform is moving away from trap-based hooks (such as Kprobes) toward highly optimized ftrace trampolines (Fprobes) and driver-level express paths (XDP).[23, 28] This shift ensures that Linux remains high-performance, programmable, and secure.[1, 28]

Source: Exploring Function Tracing with eBPF and Uprobes - Episode 1 - Anteon
URL: https://getanteon.com/blog/exploring-function-tracing-with-ebpf-and-uprobes/
Skip to main content 🎊 We've changed our name from Ddosify to Anteon! 🚀 Products Features Learn [](https://twitter.com/intent/tweet?url=https://getanteon.com/blog/exploring-function-tracing-with-ebpf-and-uprobes/&text=Exploring Function Tracing with eBPF and Uprobes - Episode 1) [](https://www.linkedin.com/shareArticle?mini=true&url=https://getanteon.com/blog/exploring-function-tracing-with-ebpf-and-uprobes/&title=Exploring Function Tracing with eBPF and Uprobes - Episode 1&summary=Exploring Function Tracing with eBPF and Uprobes - Episode 1&source=LinkedIn) [](https://www.reddit.com/submit?url=https://getanteon.com/blog/exploring-function-tracing-with-ebpf-and-uprobes/&title=Exploring Function Tracing with eBPF and Uprobes - Episode 1) ← Back Kenan Faruk Cakir Founding Engineer May 15, 2024 8 min read Exploring Function Tracing with eBPF and Uprobes - Episode 1 In this blog, we'll demonstrate how eBPF can be practically used for function tracing. eBPF is increasingly important in the field of application observability and is viewed as the future of application performance monitoring. One of the key advantages of eBPF is that it doesn't require modifying your kernel or loading kernel modules, nor does it necessary to instrument your service code for observability. We'll begin by covering the basics of eBPF and uprobes. Following that, we'll apply these concepts in a real-world example. This approach mirrors the techniques used by Anteon's eBPF Agent, Alaz, for monitoring traffic in Kubernetes clusters. All the source codes referenced in this article are available here. Prerequisites Go for attaching our eBPF program to kernel. Clang for compiling our eBPF program. Linux machine to work on. What is Function Tracing? Function tracing is a technique used in software performance analysis to monitor and record the execution of functions or methods within a program. It involves tracking the entry and exit points of functions, along with relevant contextual information, to gain insights into the program's runtime behavior. Function tracing is a powerful tool for debugging, profiling, and performance optimization. It can help identify bottlenecks, measure execution times, and analyze the flow of control within a program. Check out this article for more information on function tracing. What is eBPF? eBPF (extended Berkeley Packet Filter) is a revolutionary technology originating from the Linux kernel. It allows sandboxed programs to run in privileged contexts, such as the operating system kernel, enabling the extension of kernel capabilities safely and efficiently without modifying kernel source code or loading kernel modules.* Basically, eBPF is a super-power given at your hands and its safe due to the existence of verifier. Before an eBPF program is allowed to run in the kernel, the verifier ensures that it will not crash the kernel. For more information about eBPF verifier, refer to the official documentation. eBPF permits hooking into almost any execution point to perform tasks such as collecting metrics, traces, and conducting security checks. eBPF programs can be attached to various points, including kprobes, tracepoints, and uprobes. Let's take a closer look at these. Kprobes and Tracepoints Kprobes, short for Kernel Probes, enables developers to insert probes into the kernel code at runtime. These probes can be attached to specific kernel functions or even to individual instructions. For more information, refer to the official documentation. Tracepoints are static markers embedded within the Linux kernel code at specific locations. They serve as predefined points of interest, recording events for later analysis. Tracepoints are generally more reliable than kprobes, as kprobes may require maintenance across different kernel versions. For more information, refer to the official documentation. You can use tracepoints to track the entry and exit of commonly used system calls, such as: or to track socket states tracepoint/sock/inet_sock_set_state A list of available tracepoints can be found in this directory: /sys/kernel/debug/tracing/events/ Uprobes Unlike kprobes or tracepoints, uprobes focus on user-space instrumentation. They allow for the insertion of probes into user-space applications, rather than kernel code. Uprobes can be attached to user-space functions; when these functions are executed, the associated probe handlers are triggered. For example, you can attach a probe to a function in a Go program and track how many times it is called. Uprobes enable the dynamic instrumentation and observation of user-space program behavior by attaching eBPF handlers to any memory address, typically the entry point of a function. For more information, refer to the official documentation. Let's Dive In ! Our example involves a user-space program calling a function with randomized parameters. Our eBPF program will track how many times each parameter is called. User Program Notice the go:noinline comment, which instructs the compiler to avoid inlining the function. Since this is a simple program, the compiler would otherwise inline the function, making the function symbol invisible in the ELF(Executable and Linkable Format) binary. We compile our program with the simple command go build . We'll then run the compiled binary in a demo and expect it to be traced by our eBPF program. Finding the Attachment Point To trace the function, we need its symbol. Although it's also possible to trace direct memory addresses, we'll demonstrate using the function symbol for simplicity. To find the function symbol, tools such as nm or objdump can be used." The address and the symbol of the Greet function can be seen at the nm response. We'll use the symbol to attach our uprobe handler later. eBPF Program Our eBPF program is split into two sections. Kernel side and user-space side. The kernel side of our eBPF program is where the actual data collection takes place. This part of the program operates within the kernel space, gathering data as specified by the eBPF program's instructions. The user-space side of the program is responsible for loading the eBPF program into the kernel. After the data is collected by the kernel side of the eBPF program, it is sent back to the user-space. In user-space, this data is accessed through eBPF maps and then processed as needed. Kernel-space eBPF maps are used to transfer data collected in the kernel to user space. For more information about eBPF maps, you can refer to the kernel docs. Below, we declare the map and the event that will be sent to the user-space program for further analysis. This uprobe handler that will be triggered on each Greet function invocation. Go keeps function parameters at registers, and we capture the function parameter that we are going to count with the help of GO_PARAM1 macro. This example program is written for the x86 architecture. If you want to run it on an ARM architecture, modifications to the GO_PARAM1 macro are necessary. We compile our program into BPF bytecode using clang: > clang -target bpf -O2 -g -o tracker.o -c bpf.c The resulting tracker.o contains the compiled BPF bytecode, which will be loaded into the kernel by our user-space program in the following section. User-space The user-space program is responsible for loading the BPF bytecode into the kernel and reading data from the BPF maps populated by our uprobe handler. We use Cilium's eBPF library for this. Demo On the left terminal, our example program runs and every second it selects a random name and greets. On the right, our eBPF program runs and traces the Greet function. Keeping track of the invocation times of the function parameters and listing them on console.
Code for example programs can be found at here. The Wrap-up In this article, we've leveraged the power of eBPF to trace a function in our Go program. We've only just begun to scratch the surface in this blog post. In Episode 2, I plan to delve deeper into uprobe topics, including attaching directly to instructions and the return points of functions. On the Anteon Platform, our eBPF Agent, Alaz, utilizes eBPF to gather insights and collect observability data from Kubernetes clusters. The technique demonstrated in this blog is actually employed in capturing encrypted traffic. Don't forget to drop a star and feel free to ask your questions on any platform!
